        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1106130624" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13585398861902734470">While I love A Girl of the Limberlost, and consider one of GSP's best books. Some reviews say it's her best,but I disagree. Her best is Freckles. There are similarities in both books and you will enjoy both, but I suggest that you read Freckles first. <br/><br/>I first read this book as a pre teen and fell in love with it, and after re reading it many times I've never changed my mind. <br/><br/>Gene Stratton Porter wrote this book when she was at the peak of her writing skills and it shows. It's a story about a you</span>
  <span id="freeText13585398861902734470" style="display:none">While I love A Girl of the Limberlost, and consider one of GSP's best books. Some reviews say it's her best,but I disagree. Her best is Freckles. There are similarities in both books and you will enjoy both, but I suggest that you read Freckles first. <br /><br />I first read this book as a pre teen and fell in love with it, and after re reading it many times I've never changed my mind. <br /><br />Gene Stratton Porter wrote this book when she was at the peak of her writing skills and it shows. It's a story about a young girl's struggle against poverty, rejection, and ridicule. It's also a story bout a mother's cold and implacable hatred and the hardships Elinora must overcome to get an education. There is a love story there too, but that comes much later. <br /><br />Elnora Comstock is determined to attend college, but she soon learns that she can't afford it; tuition and textbooks are too expensive. She is ridiculed for her shabby clothes by her classmates and her crazy, malicious mother refuses to help her financially.<br /><br />Elinora soon meets a strange lady called the Bird Woman (first introduced in Freckles) who is willing to pay her money for moths, butterflies, caterpillars and chrysalides, that live and thrive in the vast dangerous swamp called the Limberlost. The Bird Woman is a naturalist and must have the specimens that thrive in the swamp.<br /><br />Elnora meets a wealthy young man named Philip Ammon, who has the same love of nature that she has, but he also has a fiancée names Edith who soon becomes jealous of Elinora. Phillip soon sees her for what she really is and the engagement is broken.<br /><br />The romance in the book doesn't really start until much later in the book. First we get to see Elinora with her classmates, making friends, learning what to wear, how to speak, etc. she begins to flower as a young woman as her circumstances begin to improve. The romance begins as a friendship and grows organically into love from that friendship.<br /> <br />What makes A Girl of the Limberlost so wonderful is the prose GSP uses to describe the natural beauty of the great swamp. You'll soon become totally engaged in the descriptions, the wonder of nature, the love story of Elinora and Phillip and you'll remember her story always. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13585398861902734470" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer53245344" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7261059809156198691">I got this as a random find on ten cent day for my local library's book sale in 2009. It was okay at first, if outdated in all sensibilities. The naturist parts were interesting. It was funny how the girl treasured the wild that was increasingly (back then even?) encroached on by society, yet she collected those rare moths to be stuck with pins (!) for profit so that she could join the society she was kept away from by her uncaring mother. Guess she wasn't too sad about it, after all. Her mother</span>
  <span id="freeText7261059809156198691" style="display:none">I got this as a random find on ten cent day for my local library's book sale in 2009. It was okay at first, if outdated in all sensibilities. The naturist parts were interesting. It was funny how the girl treasured the wild that was increasingly (back then even?) encroached on by society, yet she collected those rare moths to be stuck with pins (!) for profit so that she could join the society she was kept away from by her uncaring mother. Guess she wasn't too sad about it, after all. Her mother would not sell their acreage that would provide the money for the life her daughter needed, out of spite (her husband had been sleeping around when he died). That seemed at odds, to me. Or maybe it was just me saddened by how much was already lost. I mean, those woods and moths were her only friends for her whole life. Shouldn't it mean something?<br />I did not buy her mother's change of heart so much either. My cynical mind suspects she didn't want to be left alone, and had things stayed the same as always, they would have stayed the same. Then the story lost all interest for me when the love interest rolls into town. But that's what the author herself did: got married. That wasn't good enough reason for the same to occur in this story. Stuff just happens, I guess mostly autobiographical, in the stuff just happened storytelling way. It'd probably make a "good" Hallmark film.</span>

<div id="review_2110137714" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

        <a title="Andrea Cox" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrea Cox" href="/user/show/16407549-andrea-cox">Andrea Cox</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2110137714" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17757698321612216592">by Andrea Renee Cox<br/><br/>This was an interesting book, but it wasn't really my thing. There were positives and things I didn't much care for. I can see how it would be a fun read for others, though. I liked the idea of making one's way in the world and standing strong for what one believes in. While I found Elnora's mother annoying for the vast majority of the book, she grew on me in the latter chapters and I quite enjoyed her for a while toward the end.<br/><br/>However, I did not care for the expletive that w</span>
  <span id="freeText17757698321612216592" style="display:none">by Andrea Renee Cox<br /><br />This was an interesting book, but it wasn't really my thing. There were positives and things I didn't much care for. I can see how it would be a fun read for others, though. I liked the idea of making one's way in the world and standing strong for what one believes in. While I found Elnora's mother annoying for the vast majority of the book, she grew on me in the latter chapters and I quite enjoyed her for a while toward the end.<br /><br />However, I did not care for the expletive that was used once, the punishment of being shut in a closet (child abuse), the lead was immature for her age (hunting moths in high school and beyond, believing in "talking trees," and romping around the forest), and portions of the book were unrealistic (learning the violin without being taught, for example). There was also a scene that promoted running away from life's problems under the guise of doing a good turn for someone. That was wrong on multiple fronts, but I especially couldn't bear to see the manipulation and cowardice this portrayed.<br /><br />Overall, this was a good story, and I think many will be able to overlook the things that irritated me about it and find true enjoyment in the story line and characters.<br /><br />I was not compensated for my honest review.</span>

<div id="review_28723808" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/28723808" />
    <a title="Piebakersue" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1081610-piebakersue"><img alt="Piebakersue" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/28723808?book_show_action=true">Jul 30, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Piebakersue" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Piebakersue" href="/user/show/1081610-piebakersue">Piebakersue</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer28723808" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4173485089061309606">This was a popular book back in the very early 20th century, when it was written. It's dated but nevertheless quite interesting. The limberlost is a sort of swamp of 13,000 acres in Indiana (thank you wikipedia) and the girl, whose name I have forgotten. No, I remember, it was Elnora, lives on the edge with her crazy mother, where Elnora collects moths and excells in school. The mother is still pining the loss of her husband in the limberlost, and she resents Elnora, but truth be learned, her hu</span>
  <span id="freeText4173485089061309606" style="display:none">This was a popular book back in the very early 20th century, when it was written. It's dated but nevertheless quite interesting. The limberlost is a sort of swamp of 13,000 acres in Indiana (thank you wikipedia) and the girl, whose name I have forgotten. No, I remember, it was Elnora, lives on the edge with her crazy mother, where Elnora collects moths and excells in school. The mother is still pining the loss of her husband in the limberlost, and she resents Elnora, but truth be learned, her husband wasn't the pure soul she believed him to be... This becomes a love story, and it moves eventually to Mackinac Island, which sounds like quite the place to go in the early part of the 1900s. I'm glad I read this book for its historical significance, but I wuold only recommend it with reservations. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4173485089061309606" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2191044598" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2191044598" />
    <a title="Cathy aka The Attached Mama" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3168443-cathy-aka-the-attached-mama"><img alt="Cathy aka The Attached Mama" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1438431945p2/3168443.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2191044598?book_show_action=true">Nov 21, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cathy aka The Attached Mama" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cathy aka The Attached Mama" href="/user/show/3168443-cathy-aka-the-attached-mama">Cathy aka The Attached Mama</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11432506-a-girl-of-the-limberlost">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3168443-cathy-aka-the-attached-mama?shelf=classic-book-club">classic-book-club</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2191044598&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2191044598" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10488789863525957820">This book was enjoyable, but I felt the plot was far-fetched at times. Everything was a little "too convenient" and "too easy" for the main character, and that took away from my enjoyment. I also found the characters very one dimensional and unrealistic.<br/><br/>The story is about a young girl named Elnora Comstock who must persevere through hardships to find her "happily ever after". The problem is, Elnora doesn't really persevere for long in any area of her life. In every case of hardship, there is one</span>
  <span id="freeText10488789863525957820" style="display:none">This book was enjoyable, but I felt the plot was far-fetched at times. Everything was a little "too convenient" and "too easy" for the main character, and that took away from my enjoyment. I also found the characters very one dimensional and unrealistic.<br /><br />The story is about a young girl named Elnora Comstock who must persevere through hardships to find her "happily ever after". The problem is, Elnora doesn't really persevere for long in any area of her life. In every case of hardship, there is one flimsy coincidence after another that saves her almost immediately. <br /><br />Elnora is a poor, country girl being raised by her widowed mother. (Except we find out later she really isn't poor at all; just one of those many conveniences I mentioned earlier.) The story starts out with Elnora starting high school--- except she isn't----gasp----provided fashionable clothes and shoes!! (Oh, the tragedy!) To make matters worse, her mother sent her to school without her books and supplies knowing that they wouldn't be provided. (OK, I will accept that this was uncool.) <br /><br />Even though her dresses and shoes she wears are perfectly functional, the reader is supposed to feel *extreme pain* for the poor child because she isn't given fancy dresses, hats, and shoes. <br />Now, the story could have been saved at this point if Elnora would have had to actually **persevere** through the hardships of unfashionable clothes for a few weeks (or even days! or even another hour~) (Isn't that what the story is about after all?) Maybe the author could have had Elnora win over the affections of her classmates through her personality and character despite her unfashionable clothes...but this doesn't happen. Instead, Elnora is rescued about 6 different ways and times after enduring only 6-8 hours in her unfashionable clothes. (She doesn't even have to go to school a second time.) First, some lady in her community just happens to be offering an exorbitant amount of money for a bunch of nature artifacts that Elnora just happens to have. (So even after 6 hours of school, she finds herself sitting on a small fortune.) And Elnora just happens to see this sign advertised for the first time when leaving school on her first day. (Because, well, why not?) So Elnora has a way to pay for her tuition, supplies, and even clothing after this first coincidence. I would have granted the author this small coincidence and turn or events and went on.....But that's not all! Next, we find out that her neighbors have decided to gift everything that Elnora could have ever dreamed of having for school this same night. But that's not all! We also find out, after only this first day, her Algebra teacher has taken it upon himself to secure used books and supplies to lend to Elnora for her to pay back at her convenience. <br /><br />To Elnora's credit, she does offer to pay for all of the (many!) gifts she is given out of the blue, but even that isn't saying much since she just found herself sitting on top of a huge fortune made of moth and butterfly cocoons. My point: If you are going to write a story about persevering through hardship, at least let the character persevere a little. We aren't even left with any suspense about how Elnora will make it through school, secure her supplies, and make friends despite her unfashionable clothes. Everything is immediately solved for her through bizarre coincidences. This continues throughout the book. <br /><br />Another problem I had is that all of the characters are very flat and one-sided. Take the character of Elnora for example. Elnora just happens to be really good at everything she tries; she just happens to respond perfectly to every situation; she just happens to be perfectly self-less and thoughtful at all times, etc. etc. If Elnora ever wrestles with any character flaws, the reader is never given any hints. Anything bad that happens to Elnora is due to outside influence only. She is perfectly perfect in every way. <br /><br />Let's compare Elnora Comstock with another similar character Anne Shirley (from the 1908 novel "Anne of Green Gables" by Lucy Maud Montgomery). Both books were written around the same time. Both books have a main character who is a very smart young girl going to school for the first time. However, Anne Shirley becomes one of the most beloved characters in literature of all time.<br />Why is that? What makes Anne Shirley so much more memorable and likable as a character? How does she add to the story? I believe it is because Anne is not a perfect character. We see her fail time and time again and then have to make things right again. Anne Shirley is not perfectly-perfect. She loses her temper and smashes a child over the head with a slate on her first day of school. She insults a close friend of the family. She loses her composure and has misunderstandings. In short, she behaves like a normal person...excepts she triumphs over adversity in the end. That is how you write a story about perseverance!<br /><br />Other characters in "A Girl of the Limberlost" are just as unbelievable. We have case after case of overnight transformations. A little boy named Billy is abused and neglected, but he spends his entire waking hours looking after his little brother and sister. They seem his sole consideration. <br /> After his alcoholic father dies, however, he is happily willing to be parted from them after only a 5 minute conversation. Elnora's mother is a spendthrift and is cold hearted towards Elnora for years. However, she also undergoes a miraculous transformation after learning a single fact and then dotes over her daughter. (Oh yea, she also discovers, suddenly, she is rich not poor. And she even has a miraculous physical transformation and becomes beautiful thanks to some hair pins and a skin cream she puts on her face.) Philip, Elnora's romantic interests, is supposedly head over heels in love with another woman and has been for years. Yet, he also loses all interest in this woman (over night), and falls in love with Elnora. <br /><br />In my experience, miraculous personality transformations do not happen overnight. People are not butterflies or moths. People are not light switches who can suddenly change habits overnight. <br /><br />I give the story three stars because it was entertaining. However, I have read much better similar stories. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10488789863525957820" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3123659741" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3123659741" />
    <a title="Darlene Nichols" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/57271790-darlene-nichols"><img alt="Darlene Nichols" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577984145p2/57271790.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3123659741?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Darlene Nichols" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Darlene Nichols" href="/user/show/57271790-darlene-nichols">Darlene Nichols</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/21098923-a-girl-of-the-limberlost">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57271790-darlene-nichols?shelf=2020">2020</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3123659741" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17266631738305569798">This was a sweet story where all the characters, through different circumstances and lessons, learned to do the right thing and care about the right things. I loved how Elnora flourished as she cultivated the things in her life that made her Elnora. The swampy forest scenes with beautiful moths (who knew?!) made this story magical. One more book with honorable characters I’d love my girls to learn from. </span>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('jbGsqD/LJknHoKc0j+Z1OpFrA79FENWWZtKpHn0sODkKQh8PFvRLDI1OLGLe+SdwQWVL8MrkiM0GySwCU3qv/g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('qD7+nz+ep5RVQ2BXRn1hyd4IS+jdUJBa/dIiIKo94kEvzU04FqHK0R+t6wEXYjODDgYDp1KkzQGdyac8hGt1hg==')})
  }

Pelican Publishing Company is honored to bring this classic novel back to print as part of the Pelican Pouch series. In The Shepherd of the Hills, Wright spins a tale of universal truths across the years to the modern-day reader. His Eden in the Ozarks has a bountiful share of life\'s enchantments, but is not without its serpents. While Wright rejoices in the triumphs, grace, and dignity of his characters, he has not naively created a pastoral fantasyland where the pure at heart are spared life\'s struggles and pains. Refusing to yield to the oft-indulged temptation of painting for the reader the simple life of country innocents, Wright forthrightly shows the passions and the life-and-death struggles that go on even in the fairest of environments that man invades. The shepherd, an elderly, mysterious, learned man, escapes the buzzing restlessness of the city to live in the backwoods neighborhood of Mutton Hollow in the Ozark hills. There he encounters Jim Lane, Grant Matthews, Sammy, Young Matt, and other residents of the village, and gradually learns to find a peace about the losses he has borne and has yet to bear. Through the shepherd and those around him, Wright assembles here a gentle and utterly masterful commentary on strength and weakness, failure and success, tranquility and turmoil, and punishment and absolution. This tale of life in the Ozarks continues to draw thousands of devotees to outdoor performances in Branson, Missouri, where visitors can also see the cabin where the real Old Matt and Aunt Mollie lived.Harold Bell Wright also is the author of That Printer of Udell\'s (pb) and The Calling of Dan Matthews (pb), both published by Pelican.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"6644690912801713462\" Rebecca brings her youthful enthusiasm and imagination to their quiet life and often clashes with her stern Aunt Miranda. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10922320145678022707">She was an American author, amateur naturalist, wildlife photographer, and one of the earliest women to form a movie studio and production company. She wrote some of the best selling novels and well-received columns in magazines of the day.<br/><br/>Born Geneva Grace Stratton in Wabash County, Indiana, she married Charles D. Porter in 1886, and they had one daughter, Jeannette.<br/><br/>She became a wildlife photogra</span>
  <span id="freeText10922320145678022707" style="display:none">She was an American author, amateur naturalist, wildlife photographer, and one of the earliest women to form a movie studio and production company. She wrote some of the best selling novels and well-received columns in magazines of the day.<br /><br />Born Geneva Grace Stratton in Wabash County, Indiana, she married Charles D. Porter in 1886, and they had one daughter, Jeannette.<br /><br />She became a wildlife photographer, specializing in the birds and moths in one of the last of the vanishing wetlands of the lower Great Lakes Basin. The Limberlost and Wildflower Woods of northeastern Indiana were the laboratory and inspiration for her stories, novels, essays, photography, and movies. Although there is evidence that her first book was "Strike at Shane's", which was published anonymously, her first attributed novel, The Song of the Cardinal met with great commercial success. Her novels Freckles and A Girl of the Limberlost are set in the wooded wetlands and swamps of the disappearing central Indiana ecosystems she loved and documented. She eventually wrote over 20 books.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10922320145678022707" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
